Questioning the Analogy of the Witnessed
Baqillani challenges the analogy principle from the witnessed realm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the principle that God's knowledge must conform to the patterns observed in human knowledge.

Core Idea
The witnessed realm does not exhaust all possibilities.

The opponents argue that since all known knowledge is an accident in a body, God's knowledge must be similar. Baqillani strikes at the root of this analogy, questioning why the witnessed must be the sole criterion for possibility. This lesson presents his initial counter-arguments, including examples of things that exist without being witnessed and the challenge to deny a self-existing being.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the opponents' claim that if God had knowledge, it would have to be an accident, and Baqillani's first question: why is the witnessed realm the measure of all possibility?
Notice the phrase 'القضاء بخلاف الشاهد والوجود محال' – the opponents assert impossibility of anything outside witnessed experience.
2
Examine the counterexamples
Baqillani gives three analogies: a human not from sperm, a bird not from an egg, an egg not from a bird. These are not witnessed but are conceivable. Ask yourself how these weaken the opponents' position.
These examples are chosen to show that absence of witness does not entail impossibility.
3
Understand the challenge to deny God's existence
Baqillani then argues: if you deny God's knowledge because it is not witnessed, you must also deny a living, knowing, powerful being by itself – i.e., God – since such a being is also not witnessed. See how this reduces the opponents' position to absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the opponents' logic would force them to atheism.
4
Connect to the broader goal
Reflect on how this lesson establishes that the burden of proof is on the opponents to justify their analogy. Baqillani dismantles their foundational assumption, preparing for the next argument about the definition of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة لهم في نفي العلم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله سبحانه علم به علم؟ فإن قالوا: لأنه لو كان له علم لوجب أن يكون عرضاً حادثاً وغيراً له وحالاً فيه وغير متعلق بمعلومين على سبيل التفصيل وأن يكون واقعاً عن ضرورة أو استدلال وأن يكون مما له ضد ينفيه لأن كل علم عقلناه ثبت لعالم الشاهد المعقول فهذه سبيله؛ وإثبات علم على خلاف ما ذكرناه قول لا يُعقَل وخروج عن حكم الشاهد والمعقول وذلك باطل باتفاق. قيل لهم: ولم زعمتم أن القضاء بخلاف الشاهد والوجود محال وأن الشاهد والوجود دليل على ما وصفتم؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك متعلقاً. ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم من استحالة وجود إنسان لا من نطفة وطائر لا من بيضة وبيضة لا من طائر وفاعل فعل الأجسام؟ لأن ذلك أجمع مما لم يوجد ويعقل في الشاهد وهذا لحوق بأهل الدهر. ويقال لهم: فأحيلوا حياً عالماً قادراً لنفسه لأنكم لم تجدوا ذلك في الشاهد. ثم يقال لهم: فما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم ألا يصح كون صانع العالم جل ذكره عالماً؟ لأن العالم في الشاهد والمعقول وكل من أثبتناه عالماً في شاهدنا لا يكون إلا جسماً محدثاً متحيزاً حاملاً للأعراض مؤتلفاً متغايراً ومتبعضاً ومضطراً ومستدلاً ولا بد أن يكون ذا قلب ورطوبة وأن لا يكون الله سبحانه شيئاً موجوداً لأن الشيء المعقول لا يخرج عن أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تجاهلوا وتركوا التوحيد وإن أبوه تركوا تعلقهم بمجرد الشاهد والوجود. فإن قالوا: ليس علة كون العالم عالماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه عالماً أنه أو ذو قلب أو مستدل أو مضطر قيل لهم: وكذلك فليس علة كون العلم علماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه علماً أنه محدث عرض غير العالم وحال فيه واستحالة تعلقه بمعلومين وأنه ضرورة أو استدلال لأنه قد يشركه في جميع الأوصاف ما ليس بعلم لأن الحركة لا تتعلق بمعلومين وتقع اضطراراً أو اكتساباً وهي عرض محدث غير العالم وليست من العلم بسبيل فجاز لذلك إثبات علم على خلاف صفة ما ذكرتم كما جاز ذلك في الشيء والعالم.
A sophism for them in denying knowledge. It is said to them: What prevents you from [affirming] that God, exalted, has knowledge by which He knows? For if they say: Because if He had knowledge, it would necessarily be an originated accident, other than Him, a state in Him, not related to two known things in detail, occurring necessarily or by inference, having a contrary that negates it, because every knowledge we have conceived belongs to a knowing being in the witnessed [realm] that is intellective; such is its way. And to affirm knowledge contrary to what we have mentioned is an unintelligible statement, a departure from the rule of the witnessed and the intellective, and that is false by consensus. It is said to them: Why do you claim that judgment contrary to the witnessed and existence is impossible, and that the witnessed and existence indicate what you described? They will find no support in that. And it is said to them: What prevents you, by your reasoning, from [admitting] the impossibility of the existence of a human not from a sperm, a bird not from an egg, an egg not from a bird, and an agent that does bodies? Because all that has not existed nor is known in the witnessed, and this is adhering to the materialists. And it is said to them: Then rule out a living, knowing, powerful being by itself, because you have not found that in the witnessed. Then it is said to them: What prevents you, by your reasoning, from [accepting] that the Maker of the world, glorified be His mention, is knowing? Because the knowing in the witnessed and intellective – everyone we have established as knowing in our witnessed [realm] is only a body, originated, occupying space, bearing accidents, composite, differentiated, divisible, compelled or inferential, and must have a heart and moisture, and that God, exalted, is not an existent thing, because the intellective thing does not go beyond being a body, substance, or accident. If they pass over that, they are ignorant and abandon tawhid; if they reject it, they abandon their reliance on mere witnessed and existence. If they say: The cause of the world being knowing is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being knowing that it is of heart, inferential, or compelled – it is said to them: Likewise, the cause of knowledge being knowledge is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being knowledge that it is originated, an accident, other than the knowing being, a state in it, impossibility of relating to two known things, and that it is necessary or inferential; because what is not knowledge may share all these descriptions – for motion does not relate to two known things, occurs necessarily or acquired, and is an originated accident other than the knowing being, yet is not knowledge at all. So it is permitted to affirm knowledge contrary to the description you mentioned, just as it is permitted in the case of thing and the knowing being.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection (shubha) that God cannot have knowledge because if He did, it would have to be like human knowledge – an originated accident, distinct from Him, limited to one known object, occurring necessarily or by inference, and having a contrary. The opponents claim that any other kind of knowledge is unintelligible. Baqillani responds with a series of counter-arguments. First, he questions their principle that what is contrary to the witnessed realm is impossible; they cannot justify this. Second, he uses analogies: just as it is possible to have a human not from sperm, etc., even though not witnessed, so God's knowledge can be unlike witnessed knowledge. Third, he challenges them to deny a living, knowing, powerful being by itself (God), as that too is not witnessed. Fourth, he points out that if they insist that everything knowing must be a body with certain properties, then God would not exist, which is absurd. Finally, he addresses the objection that the definition of 'knowing' (alim) in the witnessed includes being a body, etc., by showing that the definition of knowledge (ilm) is not exhausted by its accidental properties; motion shares many of those properties but is not knowledge. Therefore, it is possible for God's knowledge to be of a different kind. The structure is a back-and-forth dialectic: each objection is met with a direct response, always using the opponents' own logic against them.

Key Terms
شبهة (objection)
شاهد (witnessed)
معقول (intellective)
عرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That whatever is not observed in the empirical world cannot exist or be true.
Correction: Baqillani shows that impossibility cannot be inferred from lack of observation; many things exist that are not witnessed, such as the Creator Himself.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani use as a reductio ad absurdum against the opponents' reliance on the witnessed?
A) A human not from a sperm
B) A bird not from an egg
C) Denying a self-existing living, knowing being
D) The materialists

Key Takeaway
The witnessed realm does not exhaust all possibilities; God's knowledge can be unlike created knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine attribute that seems unlike anything in your experience—like God's eternal, all-encompassing knowledge—do you find yourself tempted to reduce it to something familiar? This lesson warns that our limited experience is not the measure of God's reality. Will you commit to affirming God's attributes as He describes them, even when they transcend your understanding?
?
Baqillani uses three counterexamples—a human not from sperm, a bird not from an egg, an egg not from a bird—to show that absence from experience does not mean impossibility. Reflect on your own life: are there situations where you have dismissed a possibility simply because you've never seen it happen? How can you train yourself to trust in God's absolute power over the norms of nature?
?
The opponents' logic, if followed consistently, would lead to denying God's existence—a conclusion they themselves would reject. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if pushed to their logical end, would contradict your faith? Pause and examine your own reasoning, and ask God to purify your intellect so that it submits to revelation.

Redefining Knowledge Beyond Accidental Attributes
Baqillani redefines knowledge by distinguishing essential from accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani argues that the essence of knowledge is not defined by accidental properties like being an accident or limited to one object.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not defined by its accidents.

After challenging the analogy from the witnessed, Baqillani now addresses the opponents' claim that 'knowing' in the witnessed implies specific attributes like being a body. He turns the tables by analyzing the definition of knowledge itself: if knowledge were defined by those accidental attributes, then motion would also be knowledge, since it shares them. This lesson explores that decisive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Read from 'ثم يقال لهم' to the end. Notice how Baqillani shifts from questioning the principle to redefining the terms 'knowing' and 'knowledge'.
The opponents claim that 'knowing' in the witnessed always means a body with certain properties.
2
Analyze the opponents' statement about 'knowing'
The opponents argue that every known 'knowing being' is a body, originated, etc. Baqillani points out that if this definition is taken literally, it would exclude God from existence or force absurd conclusions.
They say 'the intellective thing does not go beyond body, substance, or accident'.
3
Examine the redefinition argument
The opponents then say the definition of 'knowing' is not limited to those attributes. Baqillani seizes this: similarly, the definition of 'knowledge' is not limited to the accidental attributes they listed. He gives the example of motion, which shares many attributes of knowledge (accident, originated, etc.) but is not knowledge.
This shows that the properties they listed are not essential to knowledge.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this argument clears the way for affirming God's knowledge as a perfect, non-accidental attribute. The lesson shows that God's knowledge is of a different order, not bound by the limitations of created knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة لهم في نفي العلم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم أن يكون الله سبحانه علم به علم؟ فإن قالوا: لأنه لو كان له علم لوجب أن يكون عرضاً حادثاً وغيراً له وحالاً فيه وغير متعلق بمعلومين على سبيل التفصيل وأن يكون واقعاً عن ضرورة أو استدلال وأن يكون مما له ضد ينفيه لأن كل علم عقلناه ثبت لعالم الشاهد المعقول فهذه سبيله؛ وإثبات علم على خلاف ما ذكرناه قول لا يُعقَل وخروج عن حكم الشاهد والمعقول وذلك باطل باتفاق. قيل لهم: ولم زعمتم أن القضاء بخلاف الشاهد والوجود محال وأن الشاهد والوجود دليل على ما وصفتم؟ فلا يجدون في ذلك متعلقاً. ويقال لهم: ما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم من استحالة وجود إنسان لا من نطفة وطائر لا من بيضة وبيضة لا من طائر وفاعل فعل الأجسام؟ لأن ذلك أجمع مما لم يوجد ويعقل في الشاهد وهذا لحوق بأهل الدهر. ويقال لهم: فأحيلوا حياً عالماً قادراً لنفسه لأنكم لم تجدوا ذلك في الشاهد. ثم يقال لهم: فما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم ألا يصح كون صانع العالم جل ذكره عالماً؟ لأن العالم في الشاهد والمعقول وكل من أثبتناه عالماً في شاهدنا لا يكون إلا جسماً محدثاً متحيزاً حاملاً للأعراض مؤتلفاً متغايراً ومتبعضاً ومضطراً ومستدلاً ولا بد أن يكون ذا قلب ورطوبة وأن لا يكون الله سبحانه شيئاً موجوداً لأن الشيء المعقول لا يخرج عن أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً؛ فإن مروا على ذلك تجاهلوا وتركوا التوحيد وإن أبوه تركوا تعلقهم بمجرد الشاهد والوجود. فإن قالوا: ليس علة كون العالم عالماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه عالماً أنه أو ذو قلب أو مستدل أو مضطر قيل لهم: وكذلك فليس علة كون العلم علماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه علماً أنه محدث عرض غير العالم وحال فيه واستحالة تعلقه بمعلومين وأنه ضرورة أو استدلال لأنه قد يشركه في جميع الأوصاف ما ليس بعلم لأن الحركة لا تتعلق بمعلومين وتقع اضطراراً أو اكتساباً وهي عرض محدث غير العالم وليست من العلم بسبيل فجاز لذلك إثبات علم على خلاف صفة ما ذكرتم كما جاز ذلك في الشيء والعالم.
A sophism for them in denying knowledge. It is said to them: What prevents you from [affirming] that God, exalted, has knowledge by which He knows? For if they say: Because if He had knowledge, it would necessarily be an originated accident, other than Him, a state in Him, not related to two known things in detail, occurring necessarily or by inference, having a contrary that negates it, because every knowledge we have conceived belongs to a knowing being in the witnessed [realm] that is intellective; such is its way. And to affirm knowledge contrary to what we have mentioned is an unintelligible statement, a departure from the rule of the witnessed and the intellective, and that is false by consensus. It is said to them: Why do you claim that judgment contrary to the witnessed and existence is impossible, and that the witnessed and existence indicate what you described? They will find no support in that. And it is said to them: What prevents you, by your reasoning, from [admitting] the impossibility of the existence of a human not from a sperm, a bird not from an egg, an egg not from a bird, and an agent that does bodies? Because all that has not existed nor is known in the witnessed, and this is adhering to the materialists. And it is said to them: Then rule out a living, knowing, powerful being by itself, because you have not found that in the witnessed. Then it is said to them: What prevents you, by your reasoning, from [accepting] that the Maker of the world, glorified be His mention, is knowing? Because the knowing in the witnessed and intellective – everyone we have established as knowing in our witnessed [realm] is only a body, originated, occupying space, bearing accidents, composite, differentiated, divisible, compelled or inferential, and must have a heart and moisture, and that God, exalted, is not an existent thing, because the intellective thing does not go beyond being a body, substance, or accident. If they pass over that, they are ignorant and abandon tawhid; if they reject it, they abandon their reliance on mere witnessed and existence. If they say: The cause of the world being knowing is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being knowing that it is of heart, inferential, or compelled – it is said to them: Likewise, the cause of knowledge being knowledge is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being knowledge that it is originated, an accident, other than the knowing being, a state in it, impossibility of relating to two known things, and that it is necessary or inferential; because what is not knowledge may share all these descriptions – for motion does not relate to two known things, occurs necessarily or acquired, and is an originated accident other than the knowing being, yet is not knowledge at all. So it is permitted to affirm knowledge contrary to the description you mentioned, just as it is permitted in the case of thing and the knowing being.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection (shubha) that God cannot have knowledge because if He did, it would have to be like human knowledge – an originated accident, distinct from Him, limited to one known object, occurring necessarily or by inference, and having a contrary. The opponents claim that any other kind of knowledge is unintelligible. Baqillani responds with a series of counter-arguments. First, he questions their principle that what is contrary to the witnessed realm is impossible; they cannot justify this. Second, he uses analogies: just as it is possible to have a human not from sperm, etc., even though not witnessed, so God's knowledge can be unlike witnessed knowledge. Third, he challenges them to deny a living, knowing, powerful being by itself (God), as that too is not witnessed. Fourth, he points out that if they insist that everything knowing must be a body with certain properties, then God would not exist, which is absurd. Finally, he addresses the objection that the definition of 'knowing' (alim) in the witnessed includes being a body, etc., by showing that the definition of knowledge (ilm) is not exhausted by its accidental properties; motion shares many of those properties but is not knowledge. Therefore, it is possible for God's knowledge to be of a different kind. The structure is a back-and-forth dialectic: each objection is met with a direct response, always using the opponents' own logic against them.

Key Terms
عالِم (knowing being)
علم (knowledge)
عرض (accident)
حركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does Baqillani use to show that the accidental attributes of knowledge can belong to something else?
A) A human not from a sperm
B) A bird not from an egg
C) Motion
D) A body

Key Takeaway
The essence of knowledge is not defined by its accidental properties; thus God's knowledge can be transcendent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the essence of knowledge is not defined by its accidents. But consider your own knowledge of God: have you reduced His attributes to your own limited experiences? Are you guilty of projecting your accidental limitations onto the Creator? Commit today to letting God be God in His attributes, removing your own mental constraints.
?
Baqillani shows that motion shares many attributes with knowledge but is not knowledge. In your own life, what 'movements' of the heart (desires, fears) do you mistakenly call 'knowledge'? Identify one area where you have confused an accident of knowledge (like certainty from a sign) with the essential knowledge of God. Repent and seek true knowledge.
?
The opponents relied on 'the witnessed' (shahid) to define God's attributes. Where in your life do you rely on your own experiences to define what God must be like? Confess this arrogance and ask for purification so that you may know Him as He truly is.

The Limits of Analogical Reasoning about God's Knowledge
Baqillani presents the opponent's analogy and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning from created knowledge to God's knowledge is fallacious and leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not bound by the conditions of created things.

Baqillani confronts an objection: if every learned being in our world is a body with specific properties, how can God be knowing? He shows that forcing God into these categories either leads to ignorance and abandoning tawhid or forces the opponent to abandon their own method. This lesson explores the first part of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice the opponent argues that every knowledgeable being in the visible world is a body, originated, occupying space, etc. They imply God must also be such.
3
Examine Baqillani's initial response
Baqillani presents two outcomes if the opponent insists on their analogy: either they become ignorant and abandon tawhid, or they abandon their reliance on the visible world alone.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: Why does Baqillani see these as the only options? What does this say about the limits of analogical reasoning?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : فما أنكرتم على اعتلالكم ألا يصح كون صانع العالم جل ذكره عالماً؟ لأن العالم في الشاهد والمعقول وكل من أثبتناه عالماً في شاهدنا لا يكون إلا جسماً مُحْدَثاً مُتحيّزاً حاملاً للأعراض مؤتلفاً مُتغايراً ومُتَبَعْضاً ومُضطراً ومُستدلاً ولا بد أن يكون ذا قلب ورطوبة وأن لا يكون الله سبحانه شيئاً موجوداً لأن الشيء المعقول لا يخرج عن أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً ; فإن مروا على ذلك تجاهلوا وتركوا التوحيد وإن أبَوْهُ تركوا تعلقهم بمجرد الشاهد والوجود . فإن قالوا : ليس علة كون العالم عالماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه عالماً أنه ذو قلب أو مُستَدِلٌ أو مُضْطَرٌ قيل لهم : وكذلك فليس عِلةُ كون العلم علماً ما وصفتم ولا حده ولا معنى كونه علماً أنه مُحْدَثٌ عرض غير العالم وحال فيه واستحالة تعلقه بمعلومين وأنه ضرورة أو استدلال لأنه قد يُشركه في جميع الأوصاف ما ليس بعلم لأن الحركة لا تتعلق بمعلومين وتقع اضطراراً أو اكتساباً وهي عرض مُحْدَثٌ غير العالم وليست من العلم بسبيل فجاز لذلك إثبات علم على خلاف صفة ما ذكرتم كما جاز ذلك في الشيء والعالم.
Then it is said to them: Why do you deny, based on your analogical reasoning, that the Maker of the world, exalted be His mention, could be knowing? Because the learned one in the visible and the rational, and everyone we establish as learned in our witnessed world, is only a body, originated, occupying space, bearing accidents, composed, diverse, divisible, compelled, and inferential, and must have a heart and moisture, and that God, exalted, should not be an existing thing because the rational thing is not beyond being a body or a substance or an accident. If they persist in that, they become ignorant and abandon tawhid; if they refuse it, they abandon their adherence to mere witness and existence. If they say: The cause of the learned one being learned is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being learned that it has a heart or infers or is compelled, it is said to them: Likewise, the cause of knowledge being knowledge is not what you described, nor its definition, nor the meaning of its being knowledge that it is an originated accident not the learned one and residing in it, and the impossibility of its relation to two known things, and that it is necessary or inferential, because something that shares all these descriptions is not knowledge, since motion does not relate to two known things and occurs necessarily or by acquisition, and it is an originated accident not the learned one, and it is not knowledge at all. Therefore, it is permissible to affirm knowledge contrary to the quality you mentioned, just as that was permissible for the thing and the learned one.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a defense of a key doctrine: that God's attribute of knowledge is not bound by the conditions of created knowledge. Baqillani begins by reporting the opponent's challenge: they argue that in the visible world, every knowledgeable being is a body, originated, etc., and that God would have to be such. Baqillani points out that if the opponent insists on this, they either fall into ignorance and abandon tawhid, or they abandon their own method of exclusive reliance on the visible. He then presents a counter-argument: the opponent's own description of the conditions for 'being learned' (عالم) are not the cause or definition of being learned, so they cannot be necessary. Similarly, for knowledge (علم), the descriptions they give (accident, originated, etc.) are not the cause or definition of knowledge, because there are things like motion that share those descriptions but are not knowledge. Therefore, it is valid to affirm knowledge (for God) that does not fit their description, just as it is valid to affirm a 'thing' and a 'learned one' that do not fit the visible patterns. This reasoning establishes that God's knowledge is qualitatively different from created knowledge.

Key Terms
عالم (learned/knowing)
جسم (body)
محدث (originated)
متحيز (occupying space)
عرض (accident)
جوهر (substance)
توحيد (tawhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge must be like human knowledge because human knowledge is the only kind we know.
Correction: Baqillani shows that forcing God into the categories of created beings leads to either abandoning tawhid or abandoning the method of analogy itself.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what happens if the opponent insists on their analogical reasoning about God's knowledge?
A) They prove God's existence
B) They become ignorant and abandon tawhid
C) They affirm God's attributes positively
D) They accept both options

Key Takeaway
Relying solely on the visible world to define God leads to abandoning tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, do you subconsciously picture it as similar to a human expert's knowledge? How does this passage challenge you to purify your concept of God?
?
If you were in a debate and someone used an analogy from creation to prove something about God, would you be quick to accept it? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of trusting your own reasoning without theological criteria?
?
Baqillani warns that insisting on analogies leads to abandoning tawhid. In what areas of your life have you unknowingly compared God to creation, and what step will you take today to correct that?

The Distinctiveness of God's Attribute of Knowledge
Baqillani refutes the opponent by showing that the attributes they cite are not definitional using the motion example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the descriptions of knowledge are not definitional, using the example of motion to show that shared properties do not imply shared essence.

Core Idea
Shared descriptions do not mean shared essence.

In this crucial passage, Baqillani delivers the decisive rebuttal: the opponent's description of knowledge is not its definition. Even in the created world, there are things like motion that share all the described properties—being an accident, originated, etc.—but are not knowledge. This opens the door to affirming knowledge for God without those limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: opponent's claim, Baqillani's parallel, the motion example, and the conclusion.
2
Understand the parallel argument
Baqillani draws an analogy: just as the opponent's description of a 'learned one' is not the cause/definition, the same applies to 'knowledge'.
3
Study the motion example
Motion shares all the attributes: it is an accident, originated, not the knower, etc., but it is not knowledge. This shows that the listed properties are not exclusive to knowledge.
This is the key logical move.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the descriptions are not definitional, it is valid to affirm knowledge for God in a way that transcends those descriptions, just as we affirm 'thing' and 'learned' for God without the usual constraints.

Conditions Are Not Necessary for the Unseen
Baqillani argues that conditions observed in the visible world do not apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that empirical conditions cannot be assumed for divine attributes and how he exposes the flaw in analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
What we observe does not dictate what God must be.

Baqillani engages with opponents who rely on analogical reasoning from the visible world to make claims about God. In this passage, he challenges the assumption that the conditions found in created beings—such as knowledge requiring an accident—must also apply to the Creator. He exposes the logical gap between empirical conjunction and necessary causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the dialectical structure: Baqillani presents the opponent's position, then counters it. Identify the key terms: condition (shart), cause (illah), definition (hadd).
The passage is a back-and-forth argument.
2
Understand the key distinctions
Focus on how Baqillani distinguishes between a condition (shart), a cause (illah), and a definition (hadd). The opponent tries to save their analogy by calling descriptions 'conditions' rather than causes.
Condition does not imply necessity.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the argument: 1) Opponent requires conditions from shahid for knowledge. 2) Baqillani says that leads to requiring God to have an accident. 3) Opponent retreats to 'conditions not causes.' 4) Baqillani asks for proof; the only proof is lack of counterexample. 5) Baqillani uses the same reasoning against them.
Each step undermines the opponent's position.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this matters: God's transcendence is protected from being limited by human experience. Conditions in the visible world are not necessary for the unseen.

ثم يقال لهم : فإن كنتم على الشاهد تعتمدون وعليه تعولون فأوجبوا إذا كان الباري سبحانه عالماً أن يكون ذا علم وهذا أوجب لأنه غير مُنتَقِض أحد من طرفيه لأن كل عالم منا فهو ذو علم وكل ذي علم فهو عالم وليس كل مُحْدَث عَرَضاً غير العالم وحالاً في قلب ومما يستحيل تعلقه بمَعْلُومين على وجه التفصيل فهو علم; فإن جاز إثبات عالم ليس بذي علم، وإن كان ذلك خلاف المعقول جاز أيضاً إثبات علم ليس عرض مُحْدَثٍ حال غير العالم وإن كان ذلك خلاف المعروف في الشاهد والوجود وإن هم قالوا هذه الأوصاف هي شروط في كون العلم علماً وليست بعلة لكونه علماً ولا حداً له . قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ فلا يجدون إلى تصحيح ذلك سبيلاً إلا بأنهم لم يجدوا علماً ينفك مِنْ ذلك ; فيقال لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون جميع ما عارضناكم به في العالم من شروط كونه عالماً وإن لم يكن من حده ولا معنى وصفه أنه عالم ولا من علة كونه عالماً بدلالة أنا لم نجد ولم نعقل بيننا إلا كذلك . وإن قالوا فمن أين نعلم أن الصفة شرط في استحقاق صفة أخرى؟ وهل طريق هذا إلا أنا لم نجد أحد الوصفين مستحقاً إلا مع وجود
Then it is said to them: If you rely on and depend upon the witness, then require, since the Creator, exalted, is knowing, that He have knowledge. And this is more required because it is not violated from either side, for every knowledgeable one among us has knowledge, and every possessor of knowledge is knowledgeable. But not every originated accident not the knowledgeable one and existing in a heart and impossible to relate to two known things in detail is knowledge. For if it is permissible to affirm a knowledgeable one without knowledge, though that is contrary to reason, it is also permissible to affirm knowledge that is not an originated accident existing not in the knowledgeable one, though that is contrary to what is known in the witness and existence. And if they say: These descriptions are conditions (shurut) for knowledge being knowledge, not the cause (illah) for it being knowledge nor its definition (hadd), it is said to them: Why do you say that? They have no way to correct that except that they have found no knowledge free from that. So it is said to them: Why do you also deny that all we object to you about the knowledgeable one regarding conditions of his being knowledgeable, even if it is not part of his definition nor the meaning of his description that he is knowledgeable nor the cause of his being knowledgeable, by evidence that we have not found nor known among us except that way? And if they say: How do we know that a description is a condition for deserving another description? Is the way of this except that we have not found one of the two descriptions deserved except with the existence
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage continues the argument about analogical reasoning. Baqillani presses the opponent: if you rely on the visible world, then you must require that God have knowledge (since every known knowledgeable being has knowledge). But then he points out that the visible world also shows that not every originated accident is knowledge—so the conditions are not identical. If the opponent concedes that the descriptions are conditions (shurut) and not causes or definitions, Baqillani asks: why do you say that? The only reason is that you have not witnessed knowledge without those conditions. But then Baqillani turns the argument: by the same reasoning, the opponent's objection about the conditions for 'being knowledgeable' in the world also fails—they have not witnessed a knowledgeable being without those conditions. Finally, the opponent asks: how do we know a description is a condition? The only way is that we have not seen one description without the other. This exposes the weakness of purely empirical analogical reasoning in theology.

Key Terms
شرط (condition)
علة (cause)
حد (definition)
شاهد (witness)
معقول (rational)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conditions observed in the visible world must apply to God.
Correction: Baqillani shows that lack of a counterexample does not prove necessity; empirical conditions cannot be assumed for the unseen.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani claim is the only reason the opponent considers certain descriptions as conditions?
A) Logical necessity
B) Revelation
C) Not finding a counterexample in the visible world
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
Empirical conditions do not prove necessity; God's attributes are beyond our observational limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted that God must act in a way that matches your expectations because 'that's how it always works' in your experience? What specific belief do you need to surrender to God's absolute freedom?
?
Baqillani shows that lack of a counterexample does not prove necessity. Where in your life have you treated your limited experience as a proof of how God must be? What would it look like to repent from that arrogance?
?
You say you believe God is beyond comparison, yet how often do you judge His actions by human standards? Identify one area where you have made your own observations a condition for trusting God. Will you now affirm that He is not bound by your conditions?

The Necessity of Divine Knowledge from the Witness
Baqillani argues from the witness that God must have knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the witnessed world to argue that God must possess knowledge as a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
Every knowing being in this world has knowledge—so must the Creator.

Al-Baqillani begins with a straightforward analogy. Since we observe that every knowing being in the witnessed world possesses knowledge as an attribute, consistency demands that the Creator, who is knowing, also possess knowledge. Rejecting this leads to logical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the opening phrase addressing the opponents.
The opponents rely on the witnessed world.
2
Identify the main claim
Baqillani argues that since every knowing being in the witness possesses knowledge, God must also be described as having knowledge.
The equivalence is mutual: every knower has knowledge, and every possessor of knowledge is knowing.
3
Understand the reductio
If one denies that God has knowledge, they would have to affirm a knowing being without knowledge, which is contradictory. This shows the necessity of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : فإن كنتم على الشاهد تعتمدون وعليه تعولون فأوجبوا إذا كان الباري سبحانه عالماً أن يكون ذا علم وهذا أوجب لأنه غير منتقض من طرفيه لأن كل عالم منا فهو ذو علم وكل ذي علم فهو عالم وليس كل محدث عرضاً غير العالم وحالاً في قلب ومما يستحيل تعلقه بمعلومين على وجه التفصيل فهو علم؛ فإن جاز إثبات عالم ليس بذي علم، وإن كان ذلك خلاف المعقول جاز أيضاً إثبات علم ليس عرض محدث حال غير العالم وإن كان ذلك خلاف المعروف في الشاهد والوجود وإن هم قالوا هذه الأوصاف هي: شروط في كون العلم علماً وليست بعلة لكونه علماً ولا حداً له. قيل لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ فلا يجدون إلى تصحيح ذلك سبيلاً إلا بأنهم لم يجدوا علماً ينفك من ذلك؛ فيقال لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون جميع ما عارضناكم به في العالم من شروط كونه عالماً و إن لم يكن من حده ولا معنى وصفه أنه عالم ولا من علة كونه عالماً بدلالة أنا لم نجد ولم نعقل بيننا إلا كذلك. وإن قالوا فمن أين نعلم أن الصفة شرط في استحقاق صفة أخرى؟ وهل طريق هذا إلا أنا لم نجد أحد الوصفين مستحقاً إلا مع وجود الصفة الآخر وثبوته؟ قيل له: ليس الأمر على ما ظننته. لأنه لو كان الدليل على أن الصفة شرط في استحقاق صفة أخرى ما ذكرتم، لوجب أن نقضي على أن من شرط العالم الموجود القائم بنفسه أن يكون جوهراً قابلاً للأعراض ذا حيز في الوجود. ثم إنا لم نجد عالماً في الشاهد إلا جوهراً متحيزاً في الوجود قابلاً للأعراض وهذا يبطل التوحيد وإنما المعتمد في هذا الباب هو أن يقال: إنه إنما يعلم أن وجود شرط في استحقاق صفة أخرى إذا علمنا أن وجود إحدى الصفتين مع عدم الأخرى التي جعلت شرطاً فيها مؤد إلى ضرب من المحال والجهالات وقلب الحقائق والدلالات وما قد دل العقل على فساده وأن الضرورة تقضي بإبطاله. وذلك كوصف المتحرك بأنه متحرك بكونه موجوداً، ووصف العالم بأنه عالم بكونه حياً، من حيث علم أنه لو جاز أن يكون المتحرك بالحركة معلوماً والعالم غير شيء
Then it is said to them: If you rely on the witnessed [world] and depend upon it, then necessitate that when the Creator, exalted, is knowing (عالم), He is possessor of knowledge (ذو علم). And this is more necessary because it is not contradictory from either side: for every knowing one among us is possessor of knowledge, and every possessor of knowledge is knowing. And not every originated accident (عرض) [is] other than the knowing and a state in the heart, and what is impossible for it to be related to two objects of knowledge in detail is knowledge. Then if it is permissible to affirm a knowing one not possessor of knowledge—though that contradicts reason—it is also permissible to affirm a knowledge that is not an originated accident, a state other than the knowing—though that contradicts what is known in the witnessed and existence. And if they say: These attributes are conditions (شروط) for knowledge being knowledge, not a cause (علة) for its being knowledge nor a definition (حد) for it. It is said to them: Why do you say that? They find no way to correct that except by [saying] they did not find knowledge free from that. So it is said to them: What prevents you also from [saying] that all with which we oppose you concerning the knowing one are conditions of his being knowing, even if not from his definition nor the meaning of describing him as knowing nor from the cause of his being knowing—by evidence that we have not found nor perceived among us except thus? And if they say: How do we know that the attribute is a condition for deserving another attribute? Is the way of this except that we have not found one of the two descriptions deserved except with the existence of the other attribute and its establishment? It is said to him: The matter is not as you thought. Because if the evidence that the attribute is a condition for deserving another attribute were as you mentioned, it would necessitate that we judge that from the conditions of the existing knowing substance (العالم الموجود القائم بنفسه) is that it be a substance (جوهر) receptive of accidents, possessing location in existence. Then we have not found a knowing one in the witnessed except a substance located in existence, receptive of accidents—and this nullifies monotheism (التوحيد). Rather, the relied-upon in this matter is to say: One knows that existence is a condition for deserving another attribute only if one knows that the existence of one of the two descriptions with the absence of the other which is made a condition in it leads to a kind of impossibility and ignorance and overturning of truths and indications, and what reason has deemed corrupt, and that necessity requires its nullification. That is like describing the moving thing as moving by its being existent, and describing the knowing one as knowing by its being alive, from the standpoint of knowing that if it were permissible for the moving thing to be known by motion and the knowing one to be other than something.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument and refutation concerning the divine attribute of knowledge. It begins by addressing opponents who rely on analogy from the witnessed world (الشاهد) but deny the necessity of God's knowledge being a distinct attribute. The author, al-Baqillani, first establishes the logical equivalence: every knowing being in the witnessed world is possessor of knowledge (ذو علم), and vice versa. If one rejects this equivalence for God, they commit inconsistency. The opponents try to distinguish between condition (شرط) and cause (علة), claiming that the attributes associated with knowledge in the witnessed are mere conditions, not essential causes or definitions. To this, al-Baqillani responds by challenging the basis of their distinction: they have no proof except that they have never found knowledge free from those conditions. He then turns their argument around: if mere constant conjunction establishes conditionality, then one would have to accept that every knowing substance in the witnessed must be a body occupying space, implying anthropomorphism—which nullifies tawḥīd. Thus, the correct criterion for a condition is rational necessity: the absence of the condition must lead to an impossibility. He gives the example of existence being a condition for motion and life being a condition for knowledge, because without existence, motion cannot be conceived, and without life, knowledge cannot be conceived. This rational necessity, not mere empirical observation, determines what is a condition. The passage thus defends the view that God's knowledge is a real attribute necessary for His being knowing, without falling into crude anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
الشاهد
العالم
العلم
ذو علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's being knowing does not imply a distinct attribute of knowledge; it could be identical to His essence.
Correction: Baqillani shows that in the witnessed world, 'knowing' and 'possessor of knowledge' are distinct and mutually implicative, so the same must hold for God unless one accepts absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani in this passage, what is the relationship between 'knowing' and 'possessor of knowledge' in the witnessed world?
A) They are sometimes connected
B) They are always connected
C) They are never connected
D) They are identical

Key Takeaway
The witnessed world shows that being knowing necessarily entails possessing knowledge; thus God must have knowledge as an attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on the witnessed world every day—you trust your senses and reason. But when it comes to God, do you sometimes refuse to apply the same logical consistency? Where in your life do you accept God as 'All-Knowing' yet act as if He doesn't see the details? What one thought or habit will you change today to bring your actions in line with the truth that He has full knowledge of all things, even the hidden?
?
The passage argues that every knowing being possesses knowledge. Consider your own knowledge: you know things, and you have a mind that contains knowledge. Now think of God's knowledge—infinite and perfect. Does this truth fill you with awe or with anxiety? If you truly believed that God's knowledge of you is complete and always present, how would your next hour be different? What will you do to live in that awareness?

The Criterion for Conditionality: Beyond Mere Conjunction
Baqillani refutes conditionality based on conjunction and gives the correct criterion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere constant conjunction in the witnessed world is insufficient to establish conditionality for divine attributes, and what the correct criterion is.

Core Idea
True conditionality is known by rational impossibility, not by empirical observation.

In response to opponents who argue that the attributes of knowledge in the witnessed are mere conditions, not causes, al-Baqillani shows that their reliance on constant conjunction leads to unacceptable consequences. He then provides a sounder criterion: a condition is known when its absence leads to impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's position
The opponents claim that the attributes associated with knowledge in the witness (e.g., being an accident) are conditions (شروط) not causes (علل) or definitions (حدود).
2
Understand Baqillani's initial response
He challenges them: their only evidence is that they have never found knowledge free from those attributes. But that is insufficient.
Their proof is merely empirical constant conjunction.
3
Follow the reductio
If constant conjunction were the criterion, we would have to say that every knowing substance must be a body (جوهر) occupying space. This would lead to anthropomorphism and nullify tawḥīd.
This shows the flaw in their reasoning.
4
Learn the positive criterion
Baqillani explains that a condition is known when its absence leads to impossibility. For example, existence is a condition for motion, and life is a condition for knowledge.
This criterion is based on rational necessity, not observation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Objection: Eternal Knowledge Implies Two Eternals and the First Reply
Objection that eternal knowledge implies two eternals and al-Baqillani's first reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' argument that eternal knowledge would make it a second eternal, and al-Baqillani's initial challenge to their premise that sharing one attribute implies full similarity.

Core Idea
Sharing an attribute does not make two things equals.

This passage presents a classic kalām objection: if God has eternal knowledge, then knowledge itself would be eternal, leading to two eternals. The opponent pushes this to absurdity, but al-Baqillani starts his reply by questioning their core assumption. We'll examine the objection and the first step of his rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the opponent's argument: from God's eternal knowledge, they infer knowledge is eternal by itself, leading to two eternals that must be similars, absurdly making knowledge a god and God an attribute.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the objection relies on the premise that two things sharing a single attribute (eternality) must be similars (مثلين). This is the point al-Baqillani challenges.
3
See al-Baqillani's initial counter
He first asks why sharing one attribute necessitates being similars, explicitly disagreeing. Then he begins a second argument: 'What prevents you...' but the text cuts off, so the full reply is incomplete.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the complete response is not present in this source.

شبهة لهم أخرى فإن قالوا : لو كان الباري سبحانه ذا علم لم يزل به عالماً لوجب أن يكون قديماً لنفسه كما أن العالم به قديم لنفسه ولو كانا قديمين لأنفسهما لوجب أن يكونا مثلين مشتبهين وأن يكون العلم إلهاً حياً قادراً عالماً قائماً بنفسه وأن يكون العالم صفة غير حي ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا قائم بنفسه من حيث أشبه ما هذه صفته فلما فسد ذلك، فسد أن يكون له علم فيقال لهم أولاً : لم قلتم إن المشتركين في صفة واحدة من صفات النفس يجب أن يكونا مثلين فإنا لكم في ذلك مخالفون؟ ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم، إن كان ما قلتموه
Another objection of theirs: For if they say: If the Creator, exalted, is possessor of knowledge, eternally knowing through it, then it would be necessary that [knowledge] be eternal by itself, just as the knowing one [God] is eternal by Himself. And if both were eternal by themselves, they would have to be two similars (مثلين) resembling each other, and that knowledge would be a god, living, powerful, knowing, self-subsistent, and that the knowing one would be an attribute, not living, not knowing, not powerful, not self-subsistent, from the standpoint of resembling what has this description. So when this became corrupt, it corrupts that He has knowledge. So it is said to them first: Why do you say that two things sharing one attribute among attributes of the self must be similars? For we oppose you in that. Then it is said to them: What prevents you, if what you say [is true]...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection (شبهة) from the opponents and the beginning of its refutation. The objection argues that if God has an eternal knowledge (علم قديم), then knowledge would be eternal by itself, just as God is eternal by Himself. That would make two eternal entities, which would have to be similar and equal, implying that knowledge would be a god with divine attributes (living, powerful, etc.), while God would become a mere attribute—this is absurd. Therefore, the conclusion that God has knowledge must be false. Al-Baqillani begins his response by first questioning the premise: why must sharing a single attribute make two things similars? He explicitly disagrees. Then he starts a second line of argument (which is cut off). The passage shows a classic kalām dialectic: the opponent pushes an argument to an absurd conclusion (إلزام), and the defender challenges a key premise. The response is incomplete here, but the main move is to deny that sharing one attribute implies full equality.

Misconception: Affirming eternal divine attributes implies multiple gods.
Correction: Al-Baqillani argues that sharing a single attribute (like eternality) does not make two entities equals or similars; thus eternal attributes do not imply multiple gods.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the objection that al-Baqillani challenges first?
A) That God is eternal.
B) That knowledge is an attribute.
C) That two things sharing one attribute must be similars.
D) That knowledge could be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's first move against the objection is to deny that sharing a single attribute like eternality makes two entities fully similar.

Refuting the Claim that God's Eternal Knowledge Implies a Second Eternal Being
Baqillani states the objection and gives the first counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the objection that God's eternal knowledge would entail a second eternal being, by challenging the assumption that sharing one attribute implies similarity.

Core Idea
Sharing one attribute does not make two things similar.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani addresses a common objection against the doctrine of God's eternal knowledge. The opponents argue that if God has eternal knowledge, then both God and His knowledge would be eternal by themselves, leading to two similar eternal beings. Baqillani begins his refutation by questioning the link between sharing an attribute and being similar.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the opponents present their argument. Notice how they reason from God having eternal knowledge to the necessity of two similar eternal beings.
The opponent's argument is based on the notion of 'eternal by itself' (qadīm li-nafsihi).
2
Identify Baqillani's first response
Find where Baqillani challenges the claim that sharing one attribute implies similarity. He uses the example of blackness and whiteness to illustrate his point.
Blackness and whiteness share the attribute of being contradictory (khalīfayn) but are not considered similar.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why this analogy effectively undermines the opponent's argument. Ask yourself: what does it show about the relationship between sharing an attribute and being similar?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة لهم أخرى
فإن قالوا : لو كان الباري سبحانه ذا علم لم يزل به عالماً لوجب أن يكون قديماً لنفسه كما أن العالم به قديم لنفسه ولو كانا قديمين لأنفسهما لوجب أن يكونا مِثْلَين مُشتبهين وأن يكون العلم إلهاً حياً قادراً عالماً قائماً بنفسه وأن يكون العالم صفة غير حي ولا عالم ولا قادر ولا قائم بنفسه من حيث أشبه ما هذه صفته.
فلما فسد ذلك، فسد أن يكون له علم فيقال لهم أولاً : لم قلتم إن المشتركين في صفة واحدة من صفات النفس يجب أن يكونا مثلين فإنا لكم في ذلك مخالفون؟ ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم، إن كان ما قلتموه ذلك صحيحاً، أن يكون السواد والبياض مشتبهين من حيث كانا خلافين غيرين لأنفسهما وكان وصفهما بذلك متساوياً؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً.
ثم يقال لهم : ولم قلتم أيضاً إن الباري سبحانه إذا كان قديماً كان قديماً لنفسه وكذلك علمه ؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكونا قديمين بقدم هو قدم لهما؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري قديماً بقدم والعلم قديماً بنفسه؟ وما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون العلم ليس بقديم ولا بمحدث على قول من قال ذلك من أصحابنا؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً.
ثم يقال لهم : إن كان ما قلتموه واجباً فما أنكرتم أن يكون الإنسان مثلاً لعلمه إذ كانا محدثين لأنفسهما؟ فإن قالوا: ليس المحدث عندنا محدثاً لنفسه، بل هو محدث لا لنفسه ولا لعلة؛ فلم يجب ما سألتم قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون كل قديم وصف بالقدم من صفة وموصوف فإنه قديم لا لنفسه ولا لعلة؛ فلا يجب بذلك تماثل القديمين؟ فإن قالوا : إنما وجب أن يكون القديم قديماً لنفسه لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا قديمةً، قيل لهم : فقولوا لأجل هذا بعينه إن السواد والبياض شيئان غيران خلافان لونان عرضان لأنفسهما؛ لأنهما لا يعلمان إلا كذلك؛ وقولوا أيضاً إن كل واحد منهما واحد لنفسه؛ لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا واحدة؛ فإن مروا على ذلك، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من وجوب تماثلهما، إذا كانا مشتركين في هذه الأوصاف لأنفسهما؟ ولا محيص لهم من ذلك، وإن أبوه.
Another doubt of theirs.
For if they say: If the Creator (al-Bāri), exalted is He, possesses knowledge by which He has always been knowing, then it would be necessary that He be eternal by Himself (qadīm li-nafsihī) just as what is known by Him is eternal by itself. And if the two were eternal by themselves, it would be necessary that they be two similar (mithlayn) and resembling (mushtabihayn) things, and that knowledge be a god, living, powerful, knowing, subsisting by itself, and that the knower (al-ʿālim) be an attribute, not living, not knowing, not powerful, not subsisting by itself, because it resembles what has this description.
When that became false, the claim that He has knowledge became false. So it is said to them first: Why have you claimed that two things sharing one attribute of the essential attributes (ṣifāt al-nafs) must be similar? For we oppose you in that. Then it is said to them: Do you deny, if your statement were correct, that blackness and whiteness would be resembling because they are contradictory and different (ghayrayn) by themselves and their description in that regard is equal? They will find no escape from that.
Then it is said to them: And why have you also said that the Creator, exalted is He, if He is eternal, is eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge? And why do you deny that the two are eternal by an eternity that is an eternity for them? And why do you deny that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge is eternal by itself? And why do you also deny that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated (muḥdath), according to the statement of those among our companions who said that? They will find no escape from that.
Then it is said to them: If what you have said is necessary, then why do you deny that man is similar to his knowledge, since the two are originated (muḥdathayn) by themselves? If they say: According to us, the originated is not originated by itself, but rather it is originated neither by itself nor by a cause; so what you asked does not follow. It is said to them: Then why do you also deny that every eternal described with eternity, whether attribute or described object, is eternal neither by itself nor by a cause? Thus the similarity of the two eternals does not follow. If they say: It is necessary that the eternal be eternal by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. It is said to them: Say, for this very reason, that blackness and whiteness are two things, other (ghayrān), different (khilāfān), colors, accidents (ʿaraḍān) by themselves, because they are not known except thus; and also say that each one of them is one (wāḥid) by itself because its essence is not known except as one. If they persist in that, it is said to them: Then why do you deny the necessity of their similarity, since they share these attributes by themselves? And there is no escape for them from that, even if they refuse (abū).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection (shubha) raised by opponents regarding God's eternal knowledge. The objection states that if God has eternal knowledge, then both God and His knowledge would be eternal by themselves (qadīm li-nafsihi), leading to two eternal beings, which would make them similar and thus require that knowledge be considered a separate divine entity possessing divine attributes. The author, Imam Baqillani, responds by first pointing out that sharing one attribute does not necessarily imply similarity; he gives the example of blackness and whiteness, which share the attribute of being mutually exclusive (khalīfayn) yet are not considered similar. He then challenges the opponent's insistence on 'eternal by itself', offering alternative interpretations: both could be eternal by one shared eternity, or the Creator could be eternal by an eternity while knowledge is eternal by itself, or knowledge could be neither eternal nor originated as some theologians hold. Furthermore, he turns the argument around by asking about man and his knowledge: if the opponent's logic holds, then man and his knowledge would be similar because both are originated by themselves — but the opponent might deny that the originated is originated by itself. Baqillani then generalizes: if all that is eternal is described as eternal by itself, then any two eternals would be similar, which is absurd. The opponent may then claim that eternity by itself is necessary because the essence of the eternal is known only as eternal. Baqillani counters that by the same reasoning, blackness and whiteness would have to be similar because they are known only as colors, accidents, etc., and thereby forces a reductio ad absurdum: if the opponent concedes that, they must accept the similarity of blackness and whiteness, which is contrary to common sense. The argument systematically dismantles the opponent's position by exposing logical inconsistencies.

Misconception: Sharing one essential attribute implies similarity between two entities.
Correction: Sharing one attribute does not entail similarity; blackness and whiteness share the attribute of being contradictory but are not similar.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what example does he use to challenge the idea that sharing one attribute implies similarity?
A) The similarity of fire and heat.
B) The similarity of blackness and whiteness.
C) The similarity of God and His knowledge.
D) The similarity of man and his hand.

Key Takeaway
Sharing one attribute does not force two things to be similar; Baqillani uses blackness and whiteness to illustrate this point and refute the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Further Refutations: Alternative Interpretations of Eternity and the Analogy with Created Things
Baqillani presents alternative interpretations and a reductio argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's multiple strategies to refute the objection, including alternative ways to describe God's knowledge as eternal and a reductio ad absurdum using the example of man and his knowledge.

Core Idea
Eternity does not have to be 'by itself' for everything; there are multiple valid interpretations.

Having challenged the similarity assumption, Baqillani now offers several alternative ways to understand how God's knowledge is eternal. He also turns the opponent's logic against them by using the example of man's originated knowledge, leading to a reductio ad absurdum that exposes the inconsistency in their position.

Guided Path
1
Examine the alternative interpretations
Read the section where Baqillani presents several possibilities for how God and His knowledge could be eternal. List the alternatives he offers.
Alternatives include: both eternal by one shared eternity, Creator eternal by an eternity and knowledge eternal by itself, or knowledge neither eternal nor originated.
2
Analyze the reductio with man and his knowledge
Focus on Baqillani's argument that if the opponent's logic holds, then man and his knowledge would be similar because both are originated. What does the opponent say in response?
The opponent denies that originated things are originated by themselves.
3
Trace the final reductio
Follow Baqillani's final line of reasoning: if every eternal is eternal by itself, then any two eternals would be similar. Then he counters the opponent's defense by applying the same logic to blackness and whiteness.
The opponent is forced into a contradiction: if they insist on 'by itself' for eternity, then blackness and whiteness would also have to be similar by the same reasoning.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection (shubha) raised by opponents regarding God's eternal knowledge. The objection states that if God has eternal knowledge, then both God and His knowledge would be eternal by themselves (qadīm li-nafsihi), leading to two eternal beings, which would make them similar and thus require that knowledge be considered a separate divine entity possessing divine attributes. The author, Imam Baqillani, responds by first pointing out that sharing one attribute does not necessarily imply similarity; he gives the example of blackness and whiteness, which share the attribute of being mutually exclusive (khalīfayn) yet are not considered similar. He then challenges the opponent's insistence on 'eternal by itself', offering alternative interpretations: both could be eternal by one shared eternity, or the Creator could be eternal by an eternity while knowledge is eternal by itself, or knowledge could be neither eternal nor originated as some theologians hold. Furthermore, he turns the argument around by asking about man and his knowledge: if the opponent's logic holds, then man and his knowledge would be similar because both are originated by themselves — but the opponent might deny that the originated is originated by itself. Baqillani then generalizes: if all that is eternal is described as eternal by itself, then any two eternals would be similar, which is absurd. The opponent may then claim that eternity by itself is necessary because the essence of the eternal is known only as eternal. Baqillani counters that by the same reasoning, blackness and whiteness would have to be similar because they are known only as colors, accidents, etc., and thereby forces a reductio ad absurdum: if the opponent concedes that, they must accept the similarity of blackness and whiteness, which is contrary to common sense. The argument systematically dismantles the opponent's position by exposing logical inconsistencies.

The Opening Challenge: Alternatives to 'Eternal by Self'
Baqillani presents analogies and alternatives to challenge the necessity of 'eternal by itself'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani opens the refutation by presenting alternative possibilities for God's eternity and His knowledge, challenging the opponent's assumption of necessity.

Core Idea
The opponent's claim of 'eternal by itself' is not the only possibility; other logical options exist.

In this passage, Baqillani begins his dialectical assault on the claim that if God is eternal, He must be eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge. He first draws an analogy with black and white to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to problematic conclusions. Then he presents a series of 'what prevents you from saying' alternatives, forcing the opponent to consider that eternity could be by an attribute, or that knowledge might be neither eternal nor originated. This sets the stage for the deeper refutation to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial analogy
Notice how Baqillani starts with a question about black and white being alike in being different by themselves. This is a strategic move to force consistency.
The opponent likely concedes this point, creating a starting premise.
2
Follow the hypothetical alternatives
Baqillani then asks three 'what prevents you from saying' alternatives: that both God and His knowledge are eternal by a single eternity; that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge eternal by itself; and that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated. Note the opponent cannot escape these.
These alternatives show that the opponent's claim is not the only logical option.
3
Reflect on the strategic purpose
Consider why Baqillani presents these alternatives. He is not committing to them but using them to undermine the necessity of the opponent's position.
This is a classic dialectical method: presenting multiple possible explanations to show the opponent's is not forced.

ذلك صحيحاً، أن يكون السواد والبياض مشتبهين من حيث كانا خلافين غيرين لأنفسهما وكان وصفهما بذلك متساوياً؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً . ثم يقال لهم : ولم قلتم أيضاً إن الباري سبحانه إذا كان قديماً كان قديماً لنفسه وكذلك علمه ؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكونا قديمين بقدم هو قدم لهما؟ وما أنكرتم أن يكون الباري قديماً بقدم والعلم قديماً بنفسه؟ . وما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون العلم ليس بقديم ولا بمحدث على قول من قال ذلك من أصحابنا؟ فلا يجدون لذلك مدفعاً . ثم يقال لهم : إن كان ما قلتموه واجباً فما أنكرتم أن يكون الإنسان مثلاً لعلمه إذ كانا محدثين لأنفسهما؟ فإن قالوا: ليس المحدث عندنا محدثاً لنفسه ، بل هو محدث لا لنفسه ولا لعلة؛ فلم يجب ما سألتم قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون كل قديم وصف بالقدم من صفة وموصوف فإنه قديم لا لنفسه ولا لعلة ؛ فلا يجب بذلك تماثل القديمين؟ فإن قالوا : إنما وجب أن يكون القديم قديماً لنفسه لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا قديمةً، قيل لهم : فقولوا لأجل هذا بعينه إن السواد والبياض شيئان غيران خلافان لونان عرضان لأنفسهما ؛ لأنهما لا يعلمان إلا كذلك ؛ وقولوا أيضاً إن كل واحد منهما واحد لنفسه؛ لأن نفسه لا تعلم إلا واحدة ؛ فإن مروا على ذلك، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم من وجوب تماثلهما، إذا كانا مشتركين في هذه الأوصاف لأنفسهما؟ ولا محيص لهم من ذلك ، وإن أبوه وقالوا : إن هذه الأوصاف جارية على السواد والبياض لا لأنفسهما ولا لعلة وإن لم تعلم أنفسهما إلا عليهما ، قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون القديم وعلمه قديمين لا لأنفسها ولا لعلة وإن لم تعلم أنفسهما إلا قديمتين ؟ ولا فصل لهم في ذلك ؛ وفيه سقوط ما عولوا عليه .
That is correct: that black and white are alike in that they are two different, other [things] in themselves, and their description with that is equal? They will find no escape from that. Then it is said to them: Why did you also say that the Creator, exalted, if He is eternal, then He is eternal by Himself, and likewise His knowledge? And what prevents you from saying that both are eternal by an eternity that is an eternity for them both? And what prevents you from saying that the Creator is eternal by an eternity and knowledge is eternal by itself? And what prevents you also from saying that knowledge is neither eternal nor originated, according to the one who said that among our fellows? They will find no escape from that. Then it is said to them: If what you said is necessary, then what prevents you from saying that a human, for example, is [eternal]? for his knowledge, since both are originated by themselves? If they say: The originated, according to us, is not originated by itself, but rather it is originated neither by itself nor by a cause; so what you asked does not follow. It is said to them: So what prevents you also from saying that every eternal described with eternity, whether attribute or subject, is eternal neither by itself nor by a cause; so by that, the likeness of the two eternals is not necessitated? If they say: It is only necessary that the eternal is eternal by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. It is said to them: So say, for this very reason, that black and white are two things, other, different, two colors, two accidents by themselves, because they are not known except as such; and say also that each one of them is one by itself, because its essence is not known except as one. If they pass over that, it is said to them: So what prevents you from necessitating their likeness, since they share these descriptions by themselves? They have no escape from that, even if they refuse and say: These descriptions apply to black and white neither by themselves nor by a cause, even though their essences are not known except upon them. It is said to them: So what prevents you also from saying that the eternal and His knowledge are two eternals neither by themselves nor by a cause, even though their essences are not known except as two eternals? They have no distinction in that, and in it falls what they relied upon.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of an opponent's claim that if God is eternal (qadim), He must be eternal by Himself (li-nafsihi), and likewise His knowledge. The author (Baqillani) begins by drawing an analogy with black and white, arguing that they are distinct by themselves and yet share the description of being different—so if the opponent insists on 'by itself' for God, they would have to accept that black and white are also 'by themselves' in being different, which leads to problems. He then presents a series of hypothetical objections and answers: first, if the opponent says the Creator is eternal by Himself, then why not say His knowledge is also eternal by itself? Or why not say both are eternal by a single eternity? The author points out that there is no escape from these questions. Next, he challenges the opponent's necessity: if they claim that the eternal must be eternal by itself, then why not say a human is eternal? by his knowledge, since both are originated? The opponent replies that the originated is not originated by itself. The author then turns the table: if the opponent admits that the eternal is eternal by itself because it cannot be conceived otherwise, then the same reasoning would make black and white 'by themselves' in being different, rendering them similar (mutamathilan). The opponent tries to avoid this by saying the attributes apply neither by themselves nor by a cause, but the author counters that the same can be said of God and His knowledge: they are eternal neither by themselves nor by a cause, yet known as eternal. Thus the opponent's position collapses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that 'eternal by itself' is the only logical way for something to be eternal.
Correction: Baqillani shows that multiple logical alternatives exist (e.g., by an attribute, or neither eternal nor originated), so the opponent's claim is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative Baqillani presents regarding the eternity of God and His knowledge?
A) Both are eternal by a single eternity
B) God is eternal by Himself, knowledge by an attribute
C) Knowledge is originated
D) Both are eternal by themselves

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's opening move shows that the opponent's claim of 'eternal by itself' is not necessary; other logical possibilities exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological belief, do you ever consider that there might be other logical possibilities you haven't considered? How does that realization affect your certainty? Will you actively seek out alternative explanations before settling on a view?
?
The opponent in this passage assumed 'eternal by itself' was necessary. In your own life, have you ever held a belief about God or religion as the only possibility, only to later discover other valid perspectives? How did that change your faith? What step will you take this week to examine an assumption you hold about Allah?
?
Baqillani challenges the necessity of a claim. Are you willing to have your cherished beliefs challenged in the same way? What would it take for you to genuinely entertain alternative explanations? Will you invite someone to challenge your view on a theological matter you think is settled?

The Reductio: How 'By Itself' Leads to Absurdity
Baqillani completes the refutation with a reductio and final closure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani forces the opponent into a logical collapse by showing that the 'by itself' reasoning leads to unintended similarities between black and white and between God and His knowledge.

Core Idea
If 'by itself' is necessary, then black and white become similar, which is absurd.

In this continuation, Baqillani turns the opponent's own reasoning against them. If they insist that the eternal must be eternal by itself because it cannot be known otherwise, then the same logic would make black and white 'by themselves' in being different, and therefore similar. When the opponent tries to escape by denying that descriptions apply by themselves or by a cause, Baqillani applies the same escape to God and His knowledge, leading to the complete collapse of their position.

Guided Path
1
Trace the reductio argument
Baqillani starts with 'If what you said is necessary...' and then asks why a human isn't eternal? The opponent answers that the originated is not by itself. This sets up the next move.
2
Analyze the opponent's second argument
The opponent says the eternal must be by itself because its essence is not known except as eternal. Baqillani turns this around: then black and white must be different by themselves, making them similar.
This is the key logical thrust.
3
Examine the opponent's escape attempt
The opponent then says the descriptions apply neither by themselves nor by a cause. Baqillani applies the same to God and knowledge, showing the opponent's position collapses.
Baqillani concludes: 'They have no distinction in that, and in it falls what they relied upon.'
